PREFACE. 
  Tus little book has been prepared with a view to the wants 
of two classes of learners. Those who wish to acquire the chief 
facts of Latin Grammar without intending to pursue the study 
in a more minute way, will find here, it is hoped, a statement 
of them, simple and compendious, and at the same time sufli- 
ciently scientific to give a satisfactory exercise to the powers of 
the intellect. On the other hand, young pupils who might 
be discouraged with the apparent magnitude of the task, 
if they were required to master the contents of a complete 
grammar, will not sce before them, in this book; any labor great 
enough to alarm them; and when they have acquired the prin- 
ciples of the grammar as here stated, and then betake themselyes 
for fuller information to a more complete treatise, they will per- 
ceive that a very large part of the work has been already accom- 
plished. The most forcible objection to the use of such intro- 
ductory lessons, is that—since the pupil must always, after going 
through them, have recourse to a larger and more systematic 
work, in order to reduce his fragmentary knowledge to scientific 
completeness, and to supply those parts of the subject which 
were omitted because not of the first importance—he finds in the 
complete grammar he uses a statement of the same facts ex- 
pressed in langiiage so different, that he hardly sees them to be 
of the same import; and thus has in effect to go over a great part 
of his labor a second time in mastering the same rules in their 
new dress. This objection has no weight if alleged against the 
use of this book : for in nearly all cases the statements of fact, the 
examples of declension and conjugation, and the rules of syntax, 
are the same as those contained in the new edition of Dr. Bul- 
lions’ Latin Grammar, as revised by the author of the present 
7619 Ug
-P-A-G-E-4
iv PREFACE. 
work. This book, therefore, will serve as an easy introduc- 
tion to that Grammar, and may well be used by more mature 
pupils in connection with it, as constant reference has been made 
to the larger work for a full presentation of the facts with all 
their limitations. 
It only remains to state the peculiarities of the arrangement 
of this book. It has not been thought advisable to divide the 
matter up into formal lessons of a certain magnitude: since the 
experience of all teachers must have shown them how impossible 
it is to adhere constantly to such a distribution. Instead of this, 
the book has been furnished with a very full series of paragraph 
numbers; and teachers can thus use their own judgment of the 
capacity of their pupils in deciding how many of these shall be 
assigned for a lesson. The number of words employed in the 
exercises is very small; and it is believed that the vocabularies 
can with great readiness be committed to memory, and thus the 
advance of the pupils, through the successive stages of the book, 
will be rendered casy and interesting. The examples of the con- 
jugations have been presented altogether, and the exercises 
upon them are then given in a body; and teachers can here 
again use their own opinion as to the order in which these shall 
be studied: as the system of numbering adopted makes it per- 
fectly easy to use any order considered desirable. Before the 
long reading lessons at the end of the book, will be found all the 
rules of Syntax as given in Bullions & Morris’ Latin Grammar ; 
and if these are learned by heart, the construction of the Latin 
text will be easily comprehended by the help of the constant 
reference which is made to the rules by the paragraph numbers, 
As in the Grammar, only the long vowels are marked, leaving 
it to be understood that vowels distinguished by no mark are 
short. Lastly, the declensions and conjugations have been dis- 
tinguished from each other by the letters in which their respec- 
tive stems end: but this is not done in such a way as to prevent 
any teacher who may prefer it from adhering to the usual mode of discrimination, 
0. D. M. 
Mouzeax Laxe Sonoot, N. Y., Sept. 6, 1868.

LATIN LESSONS. 
  1.—Latin Grammar teaches. the art of spoak- 
ing, reading, or writing the Latin language with 
propriety. 
2.—It is divided into four parts : Orthography, 
Etymology, Syntax, and Prosody. 
PART I. 
ORTHOGRAPEY. 
3.—Orthography treats of letters, and the mode 
of combining them into Syllables and Words. 
4,—The Latin Alphabet consists of twenty-five 
letters, the same as those of the English Alphabet, but 
without the w. 
&5.—Of these, a, e, i, 0, u, and y, are Vowels; 
the rest are called Consonants. (Bullions and 
Morris’ Lat. Gr. 3-7.) 
6.—The union of two vowels in one sound is called 
a Diphthong. 
  
1. What does Latin Grammar teach ?—2. What are its parts?—3. What is Or- thography ?—4, How many letters in the Latin Alphabet?—0. Which ure the vowels ?—6, What is a Diphthong ?

7@.—Of the consonants, x’and z are called double 
consonants, because X is made up of c or g with s, 
and z is made up of d or t with s. 
MARKS, CHARACTERS, AND SYLLABLES. 
8.—(") placed over a vowel, shows that the syllable 
in which it o¢curs/is shore, in pronunciation; as, om- 
nibus ; (~) placed over a vowel, shows that its syllable 
is long, in pronunciation ; as, amicus ; (“) placed over 
a vowel, shows that it is eanvmon (i. ¢., long or short) ; 
as, volicris, ov volucris. 
These distinctions in pronunciation are termed the Quantity of 
vowels. 
Note.—In this book, as a general rule, only long vowels will 
be marked. If a vowel have no mark over it, it is to be pro- 
nounced short. \ 
9.—The grave accent (*) is sometimes placed over 
particles and adverbs, to distinguish them from other 
words consisting of the same letters; as, gudd, “ be- 
cause,” to distinguish it from quod, “ which.” 
The punctuation in modern Latin is the same as in English. (Gr. 8, 9.) 
10,—In every Latin word there are as many sylla~ 
bles as there are separate vowels; as, cantare, pro- 
nounced can-td-re. (B. and M. Gr. 12, 13.) 
11,—In words of many syllables the last syllable is 
called the final syllable; the last but one, the pe- 
nult ; and the last but two, the antepenult, Thus,

GENERAL RULES OF QUANTITY. 
12.—All diphthongs ave long. 
13,.—A vowel is said to be-— 
(1) long by nature ; as, prinus, “a plum tree;” 
(2) or short by nature ; as, pus, “a work ;” 
(3) or long by position (viz., when before two con- 
sonants or a double consonant); as, longus, “long;” 
vox, “a, voice ;” 
(4) or short by position (viz., when before another 
vowel); as, piws, “ dutiful.” 
X PRONUNCIATION. 
14.—Two systems of pronunciation are in use, 
called the English and the Continental. The 
fundamental principle of the English system is, that 
every Latin word is to be pronounced as the same com- 
bination of letters would be sounded if it were an Eng- 
lish word. In Latin, however, there are no silent letters, 
as there are in English. 
15.--The Continental system is greatly pref- 
erable to the English. The sound of the vowels in this 
system is exhibited in the following table: 
TABLE OF VOWEL AND DIPHTHONGAL SOUNDS. 
Short % sounds like a in Jehovah, as limit. 
Long & like a in father, as fama. 
Short & like e in met, as pétdrd. 
Long 6 like ey in they, as docére. 
Short i like i in uniform, as initas. 
_Longi likei in machine, as amicus.

CONSONANTS. 
* 16.—The Consonants are pronounced generally 
as in the English language. © and g are hard, as in the 
words cat, got, before a, 0, and w? and ¢ is soft like s, 
and g like j, before e, 7, y, ae, and oe. § has always the 
sharp sound like ss, never the soft sound like z, or like s 
in peas ; thus, nds, ripés (not nose, rupese). 
17.—Accent is a special stress or force of the voice 
on a particular syllable of a word. Thus, the words ar- 
mory, assembly, disarrange, have the accent on the an- 
tepenult, the penult, and the final syllable respectively. 
18.—But in Latin the final syllable of a word never 
has the accent. In a word of two syllables, the accent 
is always on the penult. In a word of three or more 
syllables, if the penult is long, the accent is on the

PART Il, 
ETYMOGO GY. 
  
19.—Etymology treats of the different sorts of 
words, their various modifications, and their derivation. 
WORDS. 
In respect of form, words are either Declinable 
or Indeclinable. 
20.—A Declinable word is one which undergoes 
certain changes of form to express the different relations 
of gender, number, case, etc. Those changes in Gram- 
mar are called accidents ; the word accident mean- 
ing properly something which befalls or happens to an- 
other thing. 
An Indeclinable word is one which undergoes 
no change of form in these respects. 
21,—All declinable words may be supposed to 
have unaltered forms, existing before any acci- 
dent (indicating case, number, ete.) was added; such 
forms are called stems. Thus: in the words hiems,

“storm,” vultus, “face,” the letter s is an accident 
making a case; but Aiem-, vultu-, are the stems of the 
two words. 
PARTS OF SPEECH. 
22.—The Parts of Speech in the Latin lan- 
guage are eight, viz.: 
1, Noun or Substantive, Adjective, Pronoun, Verb ; 
declined. 
2, Adverb, Preposition, Conjunction, Interjection ; 
undeclined, / 
There is no article in Latin. 
Participles, Gerunds, and Supines, belong to Verbs. 
23.—When the accidents of a Nown are given, it 
is said to be deelined. : 
When the accidents of a Verb are given, it is said 
to be conjugated. 
THE NOUN. x 
24,—A Now is the name of any person, place, or 
thing ; as, Cicero, Cicero; Réma, Rome; liber, book. 
25.—Nouns are of two kinds—Proper and 
Common. 
_A Proper Nown is the name applied to an indi- 
vidual only ; as, Cicero, Cicero; Rdma, Rome. 
A Common Noun is the name applied to all 
things of the same sort or class; as, Zéber, book. 
22, How many Parts of Speech? Name them. What part of speech is wanting 
in Latin?—23. What is “to decline”? What “to conjugate”?—24, What is a 
‘Noun ?—25, A proper noun? A common noun?

ACCIDENTS OF NOUNS. Boe 
26.—Under this class may be ranged— 
1. Collective Nouns, or nouns of multitude, which signify 
one thing which contains many individuals; as, exercitus, 
“ army.” ‘ 
2. Abstract Nous, which designate qualities ; as, bonitas, 
“ goodness.” 
ACCIDENTS OF NOUNS. 
27.—To Latin Nouns belong Gender, Number, 
and Case. : 
Gender. 
28.— Gender means the distinction of nouns with 
regard to sex. 
There are Three Genders, the Masculine, the 
Feminine, and the Neuter. 
Of some nouns, the gender is determined by their signification; of 
others, by their endings. (B. & M. Gr. 32-41.) 
GENDER ACCORDING TO SIGNIFICATION. 
29.—Names of male animals, rivers, winds, months, and moun- 
tains, are Masculine ; as vir, man; Tiberis, the Tiber; notus, the 
south wind; Aprilis, Apri!; Athos, Mount Athos. 
30.—Names of female animals, trees, and many names of coun- 
tries, islands, and towns, are Feminine ; as, soror, sister; Epirus, 
Epirus ; Delos, Delos; Roma, Rome. 
Some nouns are both masculine and feminine, and are called com- 
Mon 3 as, parens, a parent. 
31.—All nouns which are neither masculine nor feminine, are 
Neuter.

8 NUMBER AND CASE. 
GENDER ACCORDING TO ENDINGS. 
Rules under this head will be given with each Declension. 
Number. 
32,—Latin nouns have two nwmbers, the Singu- 
lar and the Plural. The Singular denotes one; as, 
puer,aboy. The Plwral, more than one; as, puerz, 
boys. 
Case. 
33,.—Casx expresses the state or condition of a 
noun with respect to the other words in a sentence. 
Latin nouns have six cases, the Nominative, Genitive, 
Dative, Accusative, Vocative, and Ablative. 
34.—The meaning and use of the cases 
must be fully learned from the rules of Syntax. At 
present it is enough to say that 
The Nominative is the case of the subject of an 
affirmation: Caesar fights. 
The Vocative is the case of address: speak, O 
Caesar. 
The Accusative is the case of the object of an 
action: Brutus kills Caesar. 
The Genitive is the case of imitation. Goto 
_ the house of Caesar, 
The Dative is the case of interest. I-give a 
crown to Caesar. 
The Ablative is the case of separation. He 
comes from Caesar,

35.—The signs of the cases are the following : 
senitive, of; Dative, to or for; Vocative, O; Abla- 
tive, with, from, in, dy, etc., as in the following 
scheme : 
  Singular. | Plural. 
Nom. a king. | Nom. kings, 
Gen. of a king. | Gen. of kings. 
Dat. to or for a king. | Dat. to or for kings. 
Ace. a king. | Ace. kings. 
Voe. O king. | Voc. O kings. 
Abl. from, with, in, or by aking. | Abl. from, with, in, or by kings. 
DECLENSION. Xx 
36.— Declension is the mode of forming the sev- 
eral cases and numbers. 
In Latin there are five declensions, called the 
First, Second, Third, Fourth, and Fifth. 
The declensions are usually distinguished from one 
another by the termination of the genitive singular ; 
thus, 
The first declension has the genitive singular in -ae. 
The second e rs in -£. 
The third . st in -is. 
The fourth = - in -iis. 
The fifth - = in -€i.

the declensions is obtained by observing their stems, 
or unaltered forms, ending in different letters. Thus,

GENERAL RULES FOR THE DECLENSION OF NOUNS. 
38.—1. Nouns of the neuter gender have the nomi- 
native, accusative, and vocative, alike in both numbers, 
and these cases, in the plural, end always in a. 
2. The vocative, for the most part in the singular, 
_and always in the plural, is like the nominative. 
8. The dative and the ablative plural are alike. 
4, Proper names for the most part want the plural. 
FIRST DECLENSION. 
39.—Latin Nouns of this declension have the 
nominative ending in a. But nouns adopted from the 
Greek have nominatives ending in 6, as, 6s.* 
40.—Final a is short, except in words not de- 
clined, and in the ablative case singular of the first de- 
clension, when it is always long. The endings —is and 
—&s in the plural are long.

LATIN NOUNS. 11 
41.—Gender.—Latin Nouns in a (stem a-) 
are feminine. But appellations of men, as nauta, a 
sailor ; names of rivers; likewise Hadria, the Hadriatic ; 
cométa, a comet; planéta, a planet; and sometimes 
talpa, a mole, and dama, a fallow-deer, are mas- 
culine, 
42.—All that part of a noun, or of an adjective, 
which precedes the termination of the genitive singular, 
is called the Theme. All that follows the theme, in 
any case or number, is called the Case-ending, or 
Termination. In the examples of Declension, the case- 
endings are printed in dark letters. 
Latin Nouns of the First Declension have the 
genitive always ending in ae, and are thus declined:

46.—A single sentence contains only a single 
statement; as, Life is short ; the Flower blooms ; Brutus 
killed Cesar. (B. & M. Gr. 616, 7.) 
A single sentence consists of two parts, the Sub= 
ject and the Predicate. 
47.—The Subject is that of which something is 
affirmed. In the sentences given above, Life, the Hlower, 
tus are subjects.” 
48.—The Predicate is that which is affirmed of 
the subject. In the sentences given above, is short, 
blooms, killed Cesar, are Predicates.    
49.—A Verb is a word which makes an affirmation 
or which tells what things do. 
&50.—Some verbs in the active voice (334) assert that 
their subject does an action which affects something 
else, which is called an object. These are called 
Transitive Verbs ; as, the boy breaks the dish, the

  62.—Note.—In Latin, the order of words in a sentence differs from 
English. The two places of chief importance in a Latin sentence are 
the first and the last ; accordingly, the subject of a verb usually begins 
a sentence, and the verb is placed last. In the above exercise, arrange 
the words in the order indicated by the numbers, and so hereafter.

  SECOND DECLENSION. 
64.—Latin Nouns of the second declension 
have in the nominative singular masculine, us, er, ir, 
neuter, um. The genitive singular ends in i. 
A few Greek Nouns have nom. sing. mase. in os, 
neut.inon. (B. & M. Gr. p. 22.) 
| 65.—The stems of all these words end in o-, and the nominative 
singular was formed from the stem by adding s, before which in most 
words the vowel o was changed into the duller sound uv. Thus, from 
servo-, slave, came nom. sing. servos, and afterwards servus. 
66.—Butif the consonant r precede the o of the stem (as in puero-, 
boy; agro-, field; viro-, man), nearly always the ending s was not 
added, but the final o was dropped. So from pucro-, genero-, viro-, 
the nominative case is puer, gener, vir (not puerus, generus, virus), But

THE THIRD DECLENSION. 
125,—Nouns of the third declension are very nu- 
merous; they are of all genders, and generally increase 
one syllable in the oblique cases. Its final letters, in 
the nominative, are thirteen, a, ¢, 7, 0, y, c,d, 1, n, 7, 8, t, 
zw. Of these, a, i, y, are peculiar to Greek nouns, and & 
to a few foreign names. 
Obs. A noun is said to increase when it has more syllables in the 
genitive singular than it has in the nominative. 
126.—The stem of most words of this declension 
ends in a consonant ; and for these the ending of the 
genitive singular is is. 
Many words, however, have stems ending in i, and 
for these the genitive ending is s, which with the i of 
the stem gives is ; and this being the same as the end- 
ing for consonant-stems, is is generally stated as the 
genitive ending for all nouns of this declension. (B. 
& M. Gr. 73-86.) 
  
125. In what letters may the nom. sing. of Declension III. end? When isa 3 
noun said to increase ?—126. What is the gen. sing. ending for consonant-stems ? 
What for i-stems? How do these come to be the same?

127.—Stems ending in the consonants ¢, g, t, 4, P, 
b, m, or v, add s to form the nominative. : 
128.—But Note.—When s is added to a stem end- 
ing in © or g, x is written for os, of gs. 
129.—When g is added to a stem ending in d or 
t, these letters are left out before s.

.-COMPARISON OF ADJECTIVES. 
224,— Adjectives have three degrees of comparison, 
the Positive, Comparative, and Superlative, 
225.—The Positive expresses a quality simply ; 
as, bonus, good. The Comparative expresses a qual- 
  
220, What case is used to express the time when a fact takes place ?—222. What 
case is used to express the time during which a fact takes place ?—224, How many 
degrees of Comparison are there? Describe the uieaning of each,
-P-A-G-E-63
RULES OF COMPARISON. z 57 
ity ina higher or lower degree in one object than in 
another, or than in several taken together. The Se- 
perlative expresses a quality in the highest or lowest 
degree in one object compared with several taken sep- 
arately; as, gold is heavier than silver; it is the most 
precious of the metals. Hence, those adjectives only 
can be compared whose signification admits the dis- 
tinction of more and less. 
226.—The Superlative is often used to express a 
very high or a very low degree of a quality, without 
implying comparison; as, vir doctissimus, “a very 
learned man;” hortus amoenissimus, “a most delight- 
“ful garden.” Thus used, it is called the superlative 
of eminence, and is commonly translated with the 
article a, or an ; when comparison is implied, the article 
the must be used. 
227,—The Comparative is always of the third de- 
clension, and is declined like mitior (184). The Super- 
lative is always of the first and second, and is declined 
like bonus (98).

THE PRONOUN. 
272,.—A Pronovn is a word used instead of a now 
There are eight classes of pronouns : 
1. Personal pronouns: ego, 1; tz, thou. 
2. Reflective pronouns: s2, himself. 
3. Possessive pronouns: meus, my; noster, ox 
4. Demonstrative pronouns: tlle, that; hic, this 
5. Distinctive pronouns: is, that one (named) ~ 
6. Relative pronouns: qui, who. 
7. Interrogative pronouns: quis, who ? 
. Indefinite pronouns: aliguis, some one. @ 
273,—The last six classes are sometimes called 
Jective pronouns, since they are often used in a; 
ment with nouns; and the first two are called : 
stantive pronouns, 
274,—In all speech three things are implied: th 
person speaking, the person spoken to, and the person 
or thing spoken of. These are called, in Grammar, th. 
First, Second, and Third persons; and the proaowxz, 
representing them are called Personal Pronouns.

Known Medea;” Alexander ille, “the illustrious Alex- 
ander.” Iste, “ that,’ refers to something near, or 
some way connected with the person spoken to, and 
__ opposite to the speaker. Avcordingly, hic is often called 
the demonstrative of the firsé person, iste of the 
second, and ille of the third.

312.—Ruz XVI. The relative qui, quae, quod, 
agrees with its antecedent im gender, number, and 
person ; as, 
Vir qui ridet, The man who laughs. 
‘Viri qui rident, The men who laugh, 
Ego qui seribo, I who write. 
Laudo té qui seribis, I praise you who write. 
313.—Exrtanatioy.—In the last of these examples it will be ob- 
served that the antecedent éz is in the accusative case, being the object 
of laudo, but the relative gui is in the nominative, being the subject of 
seribis. Accordingly, the relative pronoun always takes the case re- 
quired by the construction of the sentence in which it occurs. (See B. 
& M. Gr. 683 ff.) 
314,—The best way of ascertaining what this case 
is, in translating from English, is to substitute the ante- 
cedent for the relative pronoun, placing it among the 
words of the relative clause, so as to make the same 
sense. Thus, in the first sentence in 318, the antecedent 
of “which” is “the doves.” “The doves” must be in 
the nominative case to be the subject of “are.” Now, 
to find out what case “which” must be in, use the an- 
ay te =a

THE VERB. 
328.—A Vers is a word used to express the aet, 
being, or state, of its subject, as, Canis curril, “a dog 
runs ;” Sum, “Tam,” Sedet, “he sits.” 
329. Verbs are of three kinds, Transitive and 
Intransitive, and Attributive. (See 50-52.) 
The use of a verb in a sentence is to affirm. That of which it affirms 
is called its subject. In Latin, when the subject is a personal pronoun 
it is generally omitted. (Gr. 636.) 
A Transitive Verb expresses an act done by one 
person or thing to another, as Zabulam icit, “He strikes 
the table. It has two forms, called the Active and the 
Passive voice, (334.) 
An Intransitive Verb expresses the being or state 
of its subject, or an act not done to another, as Puella 
eurrit, “The girl runs.” It is commonly without the 
passive forms. 2 
An Attributive Verb assists and connects an attrib- 
ute with its subject, as Nix candida est, “Snow is white.’ 
INFECTION OF VERBS. 
330, To the inflection of Verbs belong Voéces, 
Moods, Tenses, Numbers, and Persons. 
331.—1. The Voices in Latin are two, Active 
and Passive. 
2. The Moods are four, the Indicative, Subjunctive, 
Imperative and Infinitive. 
3. The Tenses are six, the Present (present imper-

332. The Conjugation of a verb is the arrange- 
ment of its moods, tenses, etc., according to a certain 
order. Of these, in Latin, there are are fow?, called the 
First, Second, Third and Fourth Conjugations. 
VOICE. 
333.—Voice is a particular form of the verb 
which shows the relation of the subject, or thing spoken 
of, to the action expressed by the verb. 
The Transitive Verb, in Latin, has two voices, called 
the Active and the Passive. 
334,—1. The Active Voice represents the sub- 
ject of the verb as acting on some object; as, amo t2, 
“T love thee.” 
2. The Passive Voice represents the subject of 
the verb as acted upon ; as, amdtur, “he is loved.”

Intransitive verbs, which have no proper passive, 
since they have no object to become the subject of a pas- 
sive, are also said to be in the active voice. Their mean- 
ing may be thus shown—

MOODS. 
337.—Moop is the mode or manner of expressing: 
the signification of the verb. 
The moods, in Latin, are fows’, namely, the Indica- 
tive, Subjunctive, Imperative, and Infinitive. 
338,—I. The Indicative Mood asserts the ac- 
tion or state expressed by the verb, simply as a fact, and 
generally in an independent clause; as, scribo, “I write,” 
tempus fugit, “time flies;” or inquires about a fact; as, 
nonne seribo, “ do I not write?” 
339,—II. The Subjunctive Mood asserts the 
action or state expressed by the Verb, not as a fact, but 
only as a conception formed by the mind. It is gen-

erally used in dependent clauses in various ways, and is 
often rendered in English by “may,” “can,” “ might,” 
“should,” ete. ; as, edimus ut vivdmus, “ we eat that we 
may live.” 
340.—IIL The Imperative Mood commands, 
exhorts, entreats, or permits; as, scribe, “ write thou ;” 
to, “let him go.” 
341,—IV. The Infinitive Mood expresses the 
meaning of the verb in a general manner, without any 
distinction of person or number; as, serzbere, “to write ;” 
seripsisse, “to have written ;” ace “to be written.” 
The infinitive mood is reitlle the form in which a 
verb becomes a noun. It is of the neuter gender, 
undeclined, and is used in the nominative or the accusa- 
tive case. 
Besides these moods, the Latin verb has : 
342,.—The Gerund, whichis a verbal noun of the 
second declension, used in the neuter singular, but not 
in the nominative case. It supplies the wanting cases 
of the infinitive, and answers generally to the English 
verbal noun in ing: legendi causa, “for the sake of 
reading ;” ad arandum natus, “born for ploughing.” 
343.—The Supine, which is a verbal noun of the 
fourth declension, used only in the accusative and abla- 
tive singular: spectdtum veniunt, “ they come to see;” 
optimum facta, “best to be done.”

350.—The name imperfect has been omitted with 
the Future tense, because it is usually employed as a 
simple future, without reference to duration. 
351.—The subjunctive mood has no future tense. 
The imperative has a present and future tense. The 
infinitive has three tenses, describing the action as 
unfinished (t¢mperfect), or as finished (perfect), or 
as yet to come to pass (futwre). These are commonly 
called the present, perfect, and future infinitive. 
3852.—The participles are: 
In the active voice, the present (tmperfect), and 
the future. 
In the passive voice, the perfect. 
But besides these, the gerwndive is sometimes 
called a future participle passive. It is, however, both 
in form and use much more nearly allied to the imper- 
fect participle active.

CONJUGATION OF REGULAR VERBS. 
354,—Nearly all Latin verbs are called Regular ; 
i. ¢., they form their moods and tenses according to one 
or other of fowr models or types, which are called 
conjugations. These conjugations are usually dis- 
tinguished from each other by the ending of the present 
(imperfect) infinitive. Thus: 
First Conj. Second Conj. Fourth Conj. Third Conj. 
_Infin. endsin dre, ére, ire, ere. 
355,—The true account of the matter, however, is 
that all Latin verbs have stems which end in a, e, i, 
or in a consonant oru. We have then for the 
First Conj. Second Conj. Fourth Conj. Third Conj. 
stemsin a, e, i, aconsonant or u. 
The ending of the present (imperfect) infinitive being 
re, this is added directly to the stem-vowels of the first, 
second, and fourth conjugations, making these vowels 
long, are, Gre, ire, but is attached to stems of the 
third conjugation by the help of a connecting vowel, e, 
which is always short, thus making the ending ere.

CONJUGATION OF REGULAR VERBS. oF 
- 356.—The Complete Conjugation of Latin 
yerbs requires that not only the stem, as given above 
(which may be called the first stem), should be known, 
but also two modified forms of this, which may be called 
respectively the second and third stem. 
357 .—The First Stem is that from which all the 
imperfect tenses are made. 
The Second Stem is that from which the perfect 
tenses active are made. 
The Third Stem is that with which the perfect 
tenses passive are made. 
358.—It is only in the tenses made from the first 
stem (imperfect tenses) that there is any distinc- 
tion between the various conjugations. The perfect 
tenses of all verbs are made alike from the second and 
third stems respectively. 
359.—As these three forms of the stem are involved 
in certain forms of the verb, and may be ascertained 
from them, those forms are commonly called the prin- 
cipal parts of the verb. These are (from the verb 
amo, to love): 
. The Present Infinitive, amare, giving ama-, 
1st stem. 
2 The Perfect Indicative, amiai, giving 
amav-—, 2d stem.

ing respectively first stem, second stem, third stem ; and that the fourth 
conjugation is placed before the third, in order that the three vowel 
conjugations may appear together. 
THE FOUR REGULAR CONJUGATIONS. 
361.—The examples of the four regular conjugations are now 
given to be learned by heart. The tenses of the Passive Voice are 
arranged opposite to those of the Active; not that they may be learned 
simultaneously, but that when those of the Active have been learned, 
the Passive may be compared with them in form and meaning, and may 
thus be acquired more readily and intelligently. It will be seen that 
the paragraph numbers run continuously through the Active Voice in 
each conjugation before the Passive is reached.

362.—A complete analysis of the forms of the verb, based upon 
their derivation from their stems, will be found in B. & M. Gr. 380 ff. It 
is only necessary to observe here that the Perfect Tenses Passive 
are not made from the corresponding Active ones, but are formed (in 
all the moods) by joining the Perfect Participle Passive with the differ- 
ent tenses (Present, Imperfect, Future, etc.) of the verb sum. The 
Perfect Participle so used is a complement (239), and must agree in case, 
gender, and number, with the subject. 
For example, if we wish to turn into the passive voice the following 
sentences :— 
  (1) Mater filium amavit, The mother loved her son. 
(2) Mater filiam amavit, The mother loved her daughter. 
(3) Puer malum amavit, The boy loved the apple. 
(4) Mater filids amavit, The mother loved her sons. 
(5) Mater filias amavit, The mother loved her daughters. 
(6) Puer mala amavit, The boy loved apples. 
we must change the perfect participle amatus (améato-), to make it agree 
in gender, number, and case, with the subject. 
(1) Filius 4 matre amatus est, The son awas loved by the mother. 
(2) Filia & matre amata est, Tie daa: wi. lovee a 
(3) Malum 4 pueré amatum est, The apple was loved by the boy. 
(4) Filii 4 matre amati sunt, .The sons were loved by the mother, 
Hea = The daughters were loved by the (5) Filiae 4 matre amatae sunt, milion 
(6) Mala a puer6 amata sunt, The apples were loved by the boy.

102 LATIN LESSONS. 2 
*372.—T he infinitive with a subject. The infinitive, after another 
verb, and with an accusative before it as its subject, is translated into 
English in the indicative or potential mood; and the accusative in Latin 
is made the nominative in English; as, dicit mz amare, “he says that I 
love.” The accusatives are thus translated : 
Me, that I; nos, that we; hominem, that the man. 
T@, that thou; vos, that you; hominis, that the men. 
Illum (eum), that he ; las (eds), that they; féminas, that the women. 
Infinitives, after verbs of the present, past, and future tenses, are 
rendered according to the following rules, viz. : 
374.—Rutz I. When the preceding verb is of the present or future 
tense, the present infinitive is translated as the present indicative ; the per- Sect infinitive, as the perfect indicative ; and the future infinitive, as the 
Suture indicative, thus : 
Dicit me scribere, he says that I write, o7 am writing ; 
Dicit m2 scripsisse, he says that I wrote ; 
Dicit mé scriptirum esse, he says that I will write. 
3875.—Rure Ll. When the preceding verb is in past time (i. e., i 
the PRET perfect, or pluperfect tense), the present infinitive is trans- 
lated as the imperfect, or Bae indicative ; the perfect infinitive, as the 
pluperfect indicative ; and the future infinitive, as the imperfect subjune- 
tive, thus : 
Dixit me seribere, he said that I wrote, or was writing ; 
Dixit ma scripsisse, he said that I had written ; 
Dizit me seriptirum esse, he said that I would write. 
376.—Ruix UL. The future perfect of the infinitive with a subject, is always translated as the pluperfect subjunctive, whatever be the tense of 
the preceding verb, thus : 
Dicit me seriptiirum fuisse, he says that I would have written; 
Dixit me scriptirwm fuisse, he said that I would have written ; 
Dice me seriptirum fuisse, he will say that I would have written.

the following sentences into English, observing that dicit, “he says,” és 
present time ; dixit, “he said,” past ; and dicet, “he will say,” future,

hominés rogatirés fuisse,

VERBS IN -JO.—ACTIVE VOICE. 
426.—There are a few verbs, the present of which ends in io, 
which belong, not to the fourth, but to the third conjugation. In these, 
in some forms, a consonant-stem was strengthened by the addition of i. 
Their stems may be represented thus: cap(i-). This i, however, is only 
inserted when the endings (in the fourth conjugation) begin with a 
vowel (7. e., when two vowels come together), and, accordingly, in re- 
gard to quantity, they follow the third conjugation.

42'7.—The verbs conjugated thus are: capio, I take ; cupio, I de 
sire; facio, I make ; fodio, I dig ; fugio, I flee ; jacio, 1 throw ; pario, 
Ibring forth ; quatio, I shake; rapio, I seize ; sapio, I taste, Also 
[lacio], Z draw ; [specio], Z see ; which occur only in compounds. Be- 
sides three deponents: gradior, Zwalk ; morior, J die ; patior, I suffer. 
All of these verbs have compounds which are inflected in the same way.

Active and Passive Participles of amo. 
495.—Participles are Verbal Adjectives (i. ¢., ad- 
jectives formed from verbs) which express the action of 
a verb as a property belonging to a person or thing; as, 
Cato moriens, Cato dying. Didd moritira, Dido about to die. 
Réx oceisus, The king being slain. 
496.—Rutx XXI. Participles may govern the same 
case as the verbs from which they are formed; as, 
_ Réx cultrds tonsdrids metuens, Zhe hing fearing barbers’ razors.

VERBS IN -J0. 
547 ,.—There are a few verbs, the present of which ends in io, 
which belong, not to the fourth, but to the isd conjugation. In these, 
in some forms, a consonant-stem was strengthened by the addition of i. 
Their stems may be represented thus: cap{i-). This i, however, is only 
inserted when the endings (in the fourth conjugation) begin with a 
vowel (. e., when two vowels come together), and, accordingly, 
in regard to quantity, they follow the éhird conjugation. 
VERBS IN -J0O. 
548.—The verbs conjugated thus are: capio, J take ; eupio, I de- 
sire; facio, Imake ; fodio, I dig ; fugio, I flee ; jacio, I throw ; pario, 
Ibring forth ; quatio, I shake; rapio, I seize ; sapio, I taste. Also 
[lacio], Z draw ; [specio], 7 see ; which occur only in compounds. Be- 
sides three deponents: gradior, J walk ; morior, J die ; patior, J suffer. 
All of these verbs have compounds which are inflected in the same way. 
547. What is the peculiarity of verbs like capio? When is the é inserted? In 
what respect do all the forms follow the third conjugation? 548, What verbs are 
conjugated like capio?

DEPONENT VERBS. 
558.—Deponent Verbs are those which, under 
a passive form, have an active signification; ¢ ¢, they 
assert of their subject that he does something, not that 
something is done to him; as, Joguor, “I speak;” mo- 
rior, “I die.” They are called deponent, because they 
lay aside (déponunt) the passive meaning, though they 
keep the passive form. 
559,.—Deponent verbs are conjugated and inflected 
like the passive voice of the conjugations to which they 
  
55S. What are Deponent Verbs? Why so called? 580. Howare they con 
fugated? What forms of the active have they?
-P-A-G-E-151
OEPONENT VERBS. 145 
belong; except that they have also’ the participles, 
gerunds, supines, and future of the infinitive, like the 
active voice. The future infinitive of the passive form 
is very rare. 
560.—Having no active form, Deponent Verbs 
can have only a first and third stem, since the second 
stem is only used for the perfect tenses active,

SEMI-DEPONENT VERBS. 
&81.—There are four verbs which have their im- 
perfect tenses made in the active form, while their per- 
fect tenses follow the passive. They are therefore called 
semi-deponents. These are: 
Or THE Secoxp Consucation. 
Audeo, audére, ausus sum, To dare. 
Gaudeo, gaudére, gavisus sum, To rejoice. 
Soleo, solére, solitus sum, To be accustomed. 
Or tae Tump Consueation. 
Fido, fidere, fisus sum, To trust. 
And the two compounds of this, confido, I trust, and 
diffido, I distrust, But conf ido has rarely also the 
active form of the perfect.

PREPOSITIONS. 
587.—A Preposition is a word which shows the 
relation between a noun or pronoun following it, and 
some other word in the sentence. See 266. 
The preposition, as its name imports, stands before 
the noun or pronoun which it governs.—In Latin, 
* Verbs of trusting are followed by a dative case —B. & M. Gr, 881.

588.—1. Twenty-eight Prepositions govern the

Juxta, near, beside. 
Ob, for, on account of, before.

Per, through, during, by. 
Pone, behind. 
Post, behind, afier, since. 
Praeter, besides (passing by), beyond, 
besides, except. ri 
Propter, near, on account of. 
Secundum, along, according to, 
Supra, above. 
Trans, across, over, beyond,

591.—A is used before consonants ; ab, before vowels, and A. : s 
f e 8,and sometimes /; abs chiefly before @. B is used before conso: tia, nants, 
592.—2. Tenus is placed after its case : 2; and also cum, when joined to personal pronouns, is annexed to them, and sometimes with the relative; as, mZcwm, quibuscum, etc. Clam sometimes governs the accusative; as, clam patre, or patrem. 
593.— Prepositions are often prefiaed to other 
words, especially to verbs the meaning of which they 
generally modify by their own; thus, daco, “I lead aie 
abditco, “I lead away ;” édico, “I lead out;” addico, 
“Tlead to;” prodico, “I lead forth;” imdaco, “I lead 
in;” subdico, “I withdraw.” The preposition cum, 
“with,” is written con or com in composition; thus, 
condico, “I lead together.” For full remarks on prep- 
ositions in composition see B. & M. Gr., 476 6

IRREGULAR VERBS. 
597.—Irregular Verbs are those in which some 
of the secondary parts are not formed from the primary, 
according to the rules for regular verbs. 
The irregular verbs are six; namely, swm, eo, queo, 
volo, fero, fio. Their compounds are irregular also. 
Sum has been inflected already (258 ff.). After the 
same manner are inflected its compounds, ad-, ab-, dé-, 
inter-, 0b-, prae-, sub-, super-sum ; as, adsum, adesse, 
adfui, etc. Insum wants the perfect. 
598.—1. Possum is compounded of pot- (potis, pote), 
“able,” and sum, “I am.” It is thus inflected—the t 
being changed into g before gs, and £ being omitted 
after t: 
Possum, posse, potul, I can, Iam able,

DEFECTIVE VERBS. 
638.— Defective Verbs are those in which some 
of the parts are wanting. 
1. These three, Sdi, coepi, and memini, have no first stem, and, 
therefore, are used only in the perfect tenses; and, for this reason, they 
are called Preterite Verbs. 
639.—Coepi has a present, as well as a perfect signification ; 
and hence coeperam has the sense of the imperfect, as well as of the 
pluperfect; and coepero, of the future, as well as of the future perfect ; 
thus, coep?, “I begin,” or “I have begun;” coeperam, “I began,” or 
“Thad begun ;” coepero, “I shall begin,” or “TI shall have begun ;” 
and so on of the subjunctive. 
640.—Odi and memini have the sense only of the present, im- 
perfect, and future; as, odi, “I hate;” dderam, “I hated;” ddero, 
“T shall hate.” 
a: re 641.—2. The parts of these verbs in use are as follows, through 
all their persons and numbers ; viz. : 
Oai, dderam, ddero, éderim, ddissem, Odisse. 
Paxriciprrs, dsus, dsirus. 
Coepi, coeperam, coepero, coeperim, coepissem, eoepisse. 
PaRrTIcIPLEs, coeptus, coeptirus. 
Memini, memineram, meminero, meminerim, meminissem, meminisse. 
IMPERATIVE, memento, mementote,

quently occur, Aio, “I say ;”—inquam, “Tsay,” which is used only 
between words quoted, and never stands at the beginning ;—avé, and 
salvé, “hail ;”—cedo, “tell thou,” or “give me;”—quaeso, “I 
beseech,” originally the same .as guaero. It is used commonly as an 
interjection.

653.—Grammarians reckon only fen real impersonal verbs, and 
all in the second conjugation ; namely, 
    Decet, decuit, ete., It becomes, it became, ete. 
Libet, libuit or libitum est, ete., It pleases, it pleased, ete. 
Licet, licuit or licitum est, etc., Ji is lanful, it was lawful, ete. 
Miseret, miseritum est, etc., It pities, it pitied, ete. 
Oportet, oportuit, ete., It behooves, it was incumbent on, ete. 
. Piget, piguit or pigitum est, ete., Jt grieves, it grieved, ete. 
Poenitet, poenituit, etc., Tt repents, it repented, ete. 
Pudet, puduit or puditum est, etc., Id shames, it shamed, ete. 
Taedet, pertaesum est, etc., It wearies, it wearied, ete. 
Liquet, licuit, St appears. 
654.—Impersonal verbs are applied to any person 
or number, by putting that which stands belore per- 
sonal verbs as their nominative, after the impersonals, - 
in the case which they govern; as, 
  Placet mihi, Tt pleases me, or I please. 
Placet tibi, It pleases thee, or thow pleasest. 
Placet illi, Lt pleases him, or he pleases. 
Placet nobis, It pleases us, or we please. 
Placet vobis, Et pleases you, or you please. 
Placet illis, It pleases them, or they please. 
So, pugnatur & mé,—a t2,—ab illd, etc., “it is fought 
by me,”—* by thee,”—“ by him;” that is, I fight, thou 
fightest, he fights, ete. Hence, as the meaning of a 
transitive verb may be expressed by either the active 
or the passive voice, so, when an intransitive verb is 
translated by a verb which is transitive in English, the 
English passive form of that verb is expressed, in Latin, © 
ned assive used impersonally: thus, actively, faveo 
tibi, “I favor you;” passively, fuvdtur tibi a mé, “you 
are favored by me,” and so of others: oo. al 4 658. How many real Impersonal Verbs are there ?—654. How are Impersonal

be divided into two classes ;

comprise five heads. (See B. & M. Gr., 489 ff) 
665.—B. Subordinate Conjunctions (or 
subjunctions), which connect subordinate or sec- 
ondary sentences with primary ones, These comprise 
eight heads. (See B. & M. Gr., 495 ff.) i

INTERJECTIONS. 
668.—An Intersection is a word used in exclama-

ABLATIVE ABSOLUTE. 
669.—When a noun or a pronoun, together with a 
participle or an adjective, form a clause by themselves, 
and are not grammatically connected with the rest of 
the sentence, they are said to be absolute (loosed from 
the other words of the sentence). 
670.—Rurz XXXVI. Ablative Absolute.— 
Words used absolutely are put in the ablative case, 
called the ablative absolute; as, 
Tarquinid regnante, Pythagoras { While Tarquin was king (lit. T. 
vénit, (reigning, Pythagoras came. 
{ By pouring in darts (lit. darts hav- 
Télis conjectis, aciem perfrégérunt, ing been poured in), they broke 
| through the line. *y If nature is our guide (lit. nature _ Natira duce, errari non potest, (being) guide), no mistake can be 
made. 
671.—Exrtanation.—It is clear that the absolute clause in the 
first of these examples tells the time of the action; in the second, 
tells the means ; in the third, tells the condition or cireum- 
stances under which the principal assertion holds true; and the abla- 
tive was doubtless used in Latin for the case absolute, because that 
case by itself expresses Time, Means, or Attendant Cir- 
cumstances,

PART III. 
THE PRINCIPAL RULES OF SYNTAX. 
I, CONCORD. 
-—Concord, or agreement, is fourfold ; viz.: 
1. Of a substantive with a substantive ; 
2. Of a verb with its nominative, or subject; 
8. Of an adjective with a substantive; 4. Ofa relative with its antecedent. 6 
  A SUBSTANTIVE WITH A SUBSTANTIVE. 
677.—Rux I. Apposition.—Substantives denoting the 
same person, or thing, agree in case, and the adjunct is called an 
appositive ; as, 
Cicero orator, Cicero the orator. 
Cicerdnis dratoris, Of Cicero the orator, ete. 
Urbs Athénae, The city Athens, 
Urbis Athéndrum, Of the city Athens. 
(B. & M. Gr, 621 ff.) 
CONSTRUCTION OF THE NOMINATIVE CASE. 
678.—The nominative case is used: 
1. To express the swhject of a proposition; 
2. In apposition with another substantive in the nominative, 
or predicated of it ; 
8, In exclamations ; as, O vir fortis etque amicus 1

ber and person; as,

CONSTRUCTION OF THE ACOCUSATIVE. 179 
GOVERNMENT. 
§90.—1. Goverrment is the power which one word has over 
ancther depending upon it, requiring it to be put in a certain case, mood, 
or Lense, 
2. The words subject to government are nowns and verbs, 
8. The words governing or affecting these in their case, mood, or 
tense, are nouns, adjectines, pronouns, verbs, and words indeclinable. 
4, To the Syntax of nouns belongs all that part of Syntax relating to 
the government of case. Every thing else in government belongs to the 
Syntax of the verb. 
CONSTRUCTION OF THE ACCUSATIVE. 
691.—1. The accusative, in Latin, is used to express the d- 
mediate object of a transitive active verb—or, in other words, that 
on which its action is exerted. Tse 
2, It is used to express the object to which something tends or 
relates, in which sense it is often governed by a preposition, expressed 
or understood.

  CONSTRUCTION OF THE DATIVE. 
722.—The Dative Case is believed to have originally implied 
nearness to, or proximity. But this sense of the case is only found 
distiuctly in one construction. Since, however, those things which are 
near to us affect us, and are of interest to us, the Dative came to be 
used to express this more general idea, which is, in English, denoted 
commonly by the prepositions to or for, and these are consequently 
called the signs of the Dative. (B. & M. Gr., 814 if)

CONSTRUCTION OF THE ABLATIVE. 
737.—The ablative case is used in senses very different from 
each other. Thus, in the sentences “he lived in a eave,” “he came 
out of a cave,” the word cave would in Latin be in both in the ablative 
case. In the latter, it would be a true ablative (or removal-case), ex- 
pressing separation from ; but in the former its meaning is rouch more 
like that of the dative, if the dative was correctly explained as originally 
implying nearness to. The best authorities, therefore, believe that the 
ablative, as we find it, is the result of a confusion between the dative 
and a true ablative, ending in d, which existed in the oldest period of 
the language. 
The ablative case has two leading uses ; it denotes— 
I. Various conditions of an action, as manner, cause, in- 
strument, time, place, and attendant circumstances. 
Il. Separation from. ° (B. & M. Gr., 872.)

THE SUBJUNCTIVE MOOD, 
7y94.--The Subjunctive Mood is used when the writer 
wishes to represent that he is not stating a fact, but a thowght ot 
conception. 
TENSES OF THE SUBJUNCTIVE MOOD. 
%95.—The Subjunctive Mood has foes tenses, two for incom- 
plete action (Zmperfect tenses), and two for complete action 
(Perfect tenses), these having referenée to present and past time 
respectively. Thus we have: 
(1.) For incomplete action, the Present (Present Imperfect): the Imperfect (Past Imperfect) : rem. 
(2.) For complete action, the Perfect (Present Perfect): scripserim. 
the Pluperfect (Past Perfect): scripsissem. 
  796.—The particular tense of the subjunctive to be employed in 
each case is determined by the tense of the principal verb with which it 
is connected. In this point of view, the tenses of the Indica- 
tive Mood are arranged in two classes. 
I. Tenses not Past : i. e., those which speak of a fact as pres- 
ent or future. These are often called Principal tenses. They 
are: the Present (Present Imperfect), the Perfect Definite 
(Present Perfect), the Future, and the Future 
Perfect. 
Il. Tenses Pasé: i. ¢., those which speak of a-fact as past. 
These are often called Historical tenses. They are: the Imper- 
feet (Past Imperfect), the Perfect Indefinite (Aorist), and 
the Pluperfect (Past Perfect). 
Accordingly, we have the following Rule for 
The Sequence of Tenses. 
797.—Rurz LXXXIV. TDenses not Past are followed by 
Present Tenses of the Subjunctive. Tenses Past are followed 
by Past Tenses of the Subjunctive.    (B. & M. Gr,, 1162 ff.)

809.—A Conditional Secondary Sentence is one which 
states a condition or circumstance under which something else holds 
true. The Secondary clause, which contains the condition, is called 
the Condition (or Protasis), and the Primary clause, which 
states what does or would follow from it, is called the Conclusion 
(or Apodosis). The two clauses together form a whole, which is called 
the Hypothetical Period. Thus, Si bellum omitiimus, pace 
nunquam fruémur, “If we give up the war, we shall never enjoy 
peace,” is a hypothetical period, of which s? bellum omittimus is the 
condition, and nunguam pace fruemur is the conclusion. 
(B. & M. Gr., 1259 ff)

THE USE OF THE PARTICIPLES. 
822.—Participles, being Verbal Adjectives, express the ac- 
tion of the Verb asa property belonging to a person or thing, and there- 
fore agree with nouns while they may govern cases, They have no 
distinct forms to express Time, but represent the action spoken of as 
being either finished (Perfect), unfinished (Imperfect), or yet to 
come to pass (Ftwre), at the time denoted by the verb of the sen- 
tence in which they occur, in just the same way as the forms of the In- 
finitive Mood do, 
(B. & M. Gr., 1843 ff.) 
Participles are used cither Relatively or Absotutely. 
823.—A. The Relative (Conjunctive) Participle is found 
in any case in agreement with one of the nouns which enter into the 
construction of the sentence, with the subject, the object, the limiting 
genitive, ete.: Aristides, patria pulsus, Lacedaemonem fagit, “ Aristides, 
being banished from his country, fled to Lacedaemon;” Alexandra 
9* 
’
-P-A-G-E-208
202 LATIN LESSONS. 
coelestzs honores coneupiscenti non deerat adilatio, “To Alexander, cov- 
eting divine honors, flattery was not wanting;” Hannibal Gracchum 
in insidias inductum sustulit, ‘Hannibal destroyed Gracchus, having 
been led (by leading him) into an ambuscade.” 
824.—B. The Absolute Pariiciple is found in the Abla- 
tive case (965) in agreement with a noun which does not enter into 
the construction of the sentence: Artes innumerdbilés repertae sunt, 
docente natura, “Innumerable arts have been discovered, nature being 
the teacher;” Graect Thermepylas, advenientibus Persis, oceupaverunt, 
“The Greeks occupied Thermopylae, when the Persians were Coming.” 
825.—Ruiz CV. The Participle, whether Relative or Absolute, is 
used to express various circumstances of the action, as Time, Meams, 
Manner, Cause, Condition, etc., and is often to be rendered in English 
by a secondary sentence. ‘

Mosr of the abbreviations used are sufficiently intelligible of them- 
selves. The Roman numerals I, II., IL, IV., stand respectively for 
“verb of tho Virst, Second, Third, Fourth Conjugation.” So m., f., ., 
¢., stand respectively for “noun of the masculine, feminine, neuter, 
common gender.” If it be preferred to give the parts of the verbs in 
the ordinary way, all that is necessary is to add i to the second stem, 
and to chango o of the third stem to um. The declension of nouns 
and adjectives is indicated by the final letter of their stems.

KENDRICK’S XENOPHON’S ANABASIS, 538 Pages. 
Comprising the whole work, with Kiepert’s Revised Map of the Route of the 
Ten Thousand, introduction, full though brief Notes, and complete Vocabulary, 
by A. C. Kenrick, D. D., LL. D., Rochester University. 
BULLIONS’S 
LATIN-ENGLISH AND ENGLISH-LATIN DICTIONARY. 
(1,258 pages.) This book has peculiar advantages in the distinctness of the 
marks of the quantities of Syllables, the Etymology and Composition of Words, 
Classification of Syllables, Synonyms, and Proper Names, and a judicious 
Abridgment of Quotations, For cheapness and utility it is unequalled. 
LONG'S ATLAS. OF CLASSICAL GEOGRAPHY. 
This Atlas, by Gzorcr Lona, M. A., late Fellow of Trinity College, Cam- 
bridge, contains fifty-two Maps and Plans, finely engraved and neatly colored; 
with a Sketch of Classical Geography, and a full Index of Places. The maps, 
showing the ideas which the ancients had of the world at various intervals from 
Homer to Ptolemy, and the typographical plans of ancient places, battles, 
marches, will be of interest and advantage; and the Atlas will be of great help 
to classical students, and in libraries of reference. 
BAIRD’S CLASSICAL MANUAL. 
(200 pages.) This is a student’s hand-book, presenting, in a concise form, an 
epitome of Ancient Geography, the Mythology, Antiquities, and Chronology of 
the Greeks and Romans. 
HOOKER’S NEW PHYSIOLOGY. 
376 Pages. 
Revised, corrected, and put into the most perfect form for text-book use, by J. 
A. Sewatt, M. D,, of the Illinois State Normal University. 
This New Physiology has been Vewly Electrotyped in large-sized types 
using the black-faced type to bring out prominently the leading ideas. It 
contains a full series of Questions at the end of the book, and a complete 
Glossary and Index.

The Elements of Arithmetic. 
This is a practical treatise on Arithmetic, furnishing in one book of 308 
pages ail the arithmetic compatible with a well-balanced common-school course,

called Written Arithmetic.
